                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.201701561')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)

            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="c004e996dd7e493a97ba61383979c026"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="c004e996dd7e493a97ba61383979c026">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '64cbc4d8-d52e-43c2-bf33-e519ba830d73'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '1D16DAFD16CC073A024255055AF352E7';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

                     <p>Department of Materials Science, Fudan University, Shanghai, 200433 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Gao/Qili">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Sun/Dalin" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a3" data-db-target-for="a3" aria-controls="a3" aria-haspopup="true" id="a3_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Dalin Sun</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a3" aria-labelledby="a3_Ctrl" role="region" id="a3">
                     <p class="author-name">Dalin Sun</p>
                     <p>Department of Materials Science, Fudan University, Shanghai, 200433 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Sun/Dalin">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Yu/Xuebin" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a4" data-db-target-for="a4" aria-controls="a4" aria-haspopup="true" id="a4_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Xuebin Yu<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a4" aria-labelledby="a4_Ctrl" role="region" id="a4">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Xuebin Yu</p>

                     </ul>
                     <p>Department of Materials Science, Fudan University, Shanghai, 200433 China</p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:yuxuebin@fudan.edu.cn"><span>yuxuebin@fudan.edu.cn</span></a><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Yu/Xuebin">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d50504760" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> is regarded as a promising anode material for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its high specific capacity. The large volume change during discharge and charge processes, however, induces significant cracking of the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> anodes, leading to rapid fading of the capacity. Herein, a novel peapod-like nanostructured material, consisting of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles homogeneously encapsulated in the hollow interior of N-doped porous carbon nanofibers, as a high-performance anode material is reported. The distinctive structure not only provides enough voids to accommodate the volume expansion of the pea-like Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles but also offers a continuous conducting framework for electron transport and accessible nanoporous channels for fast diffusion and transport of Li/Na-ions. As a consequence, this peapod-like structure exhibits a stable discharge capacity of 1434 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) and 806 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (at 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) over 100 cycles as anode materials for LIBs and SIBs, respectively. More importantly, a stable capacity of 958 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles and 396 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1500 cycles can be achieved for LIBs and SIBs, respectively, at a large current density of 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. This study provides a promising strategy for developing long-cycle-life LIBs and SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201701561-sec-0010">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201701561-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Rechargeable lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted much interest as efficient energy storage technologies for integration of large-scale renewable energy and for transportation due to their high energy densities, long cycle lifetimes, and environmental friendliness.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0001_R_d50504752e382" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nat. Energy Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">1</a></span> Several challenging issues, e.g., the capacity, stability, and rate performance of these rechargeable batteries, however, still need to be overcome to fulfill the key requirements of new mid- and large-scale energy-storage applications, including high power capability, stable cycle life without explosion risks, high energy density, and cost-effectiveness.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0002_R_d50504752e384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater. Chem. Soc. Rev.">2</a></span> In this regard, iron oxide (Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>) has been demonstrated to be one of the most promising candidate anode materials because of its high theoretical capacity for both LIBs and SIBs (1007 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), nontoxicity, high natural abundance (since iron is the fourth richest element in the Earth's crust), and low processing cost.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0003_R_d50504752e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Commun.">3</a>, <a href="#smll201701561-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0004_R_d50504752e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></span> The key challenge associated with the practical application of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> for anodes in LIBs and SIBs is its rapid capacity fading during cycling, which is caused by its large volume expansion/contraction, its low intrinsic electric conductivity, and its severe particle aggregation.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0005_R_d50504752e399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - ACS Nano Nano Lett. Nano Energy Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></span> In particular, in terms of SIBs, since Na-ions (≈1.06 Å) have a larger ionic radius than Li-ions (≈0.76 Å), the reaction kinetics between anode/cathode materials and Na-ions is aggravated by more severe volume changes and larger polarization, leading to lower reversible capacities and poorer cycle life compared with LIBs.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0006_R_d50504752e401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Rev. J. Mater. Chem. A">6</a></span></p>
         
         <p>To date, many efforts have been focused on the synthesis of nanostructured electrode materials and the design of various morphologies, such as nanoflowers, nanospheres, and nanotubes, for improving Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-based materials as high-performance LIB and SIB anodes.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0007_R_d50504752e409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Commun. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater. J. Power Sources J. Mater. Chem. A">7</a></span> Although downsizing the particle size to the nanometer range can efficiently address the internal strain induced during charge–discharge processes and reduce the distances for ion and electron transport, resulting in high-rate electrochemical performance, the cycling performance is still limited due to the undesirable electrode/electrolyte reactions, the aggregation of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles (NPs), and the repeated decomposition/formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) films owing to the cyclic exposure of the active material surface to electrolyte.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0008_R_d50504752e416" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Nano Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></span> In order to solve these issues, the incorporation of nanostructured Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> in composites with conductive carbonaceous materials has been designed and the composites constructed, which can significantly alleviate the aggregation of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs, relieve the volume expansion/shrinkage during repeated insertion/extraction of Li<sup>+</sup> or Na<sup>+</sup>, and enhance the electrical conductivity of the electrode.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0004_R_d50504752e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#smll201701561-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0009_R_d50504752e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Interfaces ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Sci. Rep. J. Power Sources">9</a></span> As a result, the cycling stability of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> anodes could be enhanced up to even hundreds of cycles with relatively high capacity retention. Cracking and fracturing of the carbon shells, however, may occur during charging/discharging processes in the course of cycling owing to the lack of enough space for accommodating the large volume expansion of the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> during cycling lithiation/sodiation processes.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0010_R_d50504752e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Lett.">10</a></span> This could be the reason why the cycle life of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> dispersed in carbon matrix as anode material is still far from the value required for practical applications. Therefore, the development of highly stable Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-based anodes with a facile and general approach toward fast kinetics and long cycle life is essential and imperative for their practical applications in LIBs and SIBs, although it remains a great challenge.</p>
         
         <p>Here, in pursuing outstanding cycling stability and rate capability, we have rationally designed a freestanding, 3D composite based on Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs encapsulated in peapod-like porous carbon nanofibers uniformly doped with nitrogen, denoted as Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs, as a flexible electrode for high-performance LIBs and SIBs. This electrode is prepared via a one-step continuous electrospinning technique and subsequent thermal treatment with lithium azide adopted as both the porogen and the source of nitrogen. As illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0001">1</a>a, taking advantage of the explosive decomposition of azides and phase separation during the formation of carbon nanofibers (CNFs), abundant pores are formed in the core of the CNFs, accompanied by the formation of homogeneous Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs with an average size of 16 nm, resulting in the formation of a 0D-in-1D peapod-like structure, and the simultaneous doping of nitrogen into the CNFs. The ultrafine particle size of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs significantly enhances their electrochemical activity and shortens the diffusion lengths of lithium and sodium ions, while the large void space in the interior of the PCNFs provides sufficient space for the expansion of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> to overcome the structural damage during repeated charge and discharge processes. Moreover, the construction of self-supported electronic active materials can serve as an efficient electronic current collector, thereby eliminating the requirement for conductive additives, polymeric binders, and metallic current collectors that generally amounts to a total of ≈10% of the cell weight in the conventional negative electrode structure. This design could further enhance the power density of batteries as well as simplifying the cell packing process. As a result, this novel Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs structure can be used directly as a binder- and current collector-free electrode in LIBs and SIBs, which shows either high specific capacity (1953 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for LIBs and 1542 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for SIBs) or excellent capacity retention (1434 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for LIBs at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and 806 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for SIBs at 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, even after 100 cycles). Strikingly, a reversible capacity of 958 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles and 396 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1500 cycles for LIBs and SIBs, respectively, is observed at the high rate of 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> with only 0.015% and 0.007% decay per cycle from the second cycle, showing remarkable long-term cycling stability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201701561-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/75e5ab22-d501-416c-8f52-a6bfb8c4e265/smll201701561-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>

      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201701561-sec-0020">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201701561-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll201701561-sec-0030">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll201701561-sec-0030-title">2.1 Synthesis and Structural Characterization of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The synthesis process for the freestanding Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode is schematically illustrated in Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0001">1</a>a. In a typical procedure, a precursor consisting of poly(vinyl alcohol) (PVA), LiN<sub>3</sub>, and FeCl<sub>3</sub> in water was fabricated into a nanofiber web via a single-nozzle electrospinning technique (Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0001">1</a>b). After drying under dynamic vacuum at 75 °C, in which LiN<sub>3</sub> and FeCl<sub>3</sub> tend to aggregate in the core of PVA nanofibers due to the phase separation during drying, the fibers were annealed in nitrogen atmosphere for carbonization to synthesize CNFs. Meanwhile, abundant mesopores and macropores were formed in the interior of the CNFs generated by the violent decomposition of lithium azide during carbonization based on the following equation with the in situ encapsulation of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs: 
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201701561-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/28cb7276-4061-4351-8f7d-bf26a4790f36/smll201701561-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201701561:smll201701561-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201701561:smll201701561-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>Finally, the freestanding films were dehydrated, annealed under oxygen for complete oxidation at 300 °C, and then cut into smaller squares as the freestanding, binder-free electrodes (Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0001">1</a>c), which were directly used as the working anodes for electrochemical measurements. Field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FE-SEM) characterization demonstrated that a typical Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs sample exhibits a long, continuous, and interconnected structure with an average CNF diameter of about 200 nm (Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0001">1</a>d,e), which is beneficial for the penetration of electrolyte and fast ion transport. As seen from the side-view SEM image of the fibrous web (Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0001">1</a>d), the thickness of the freestanding electrode is measured to be about 35 µm.</p>
            
            <p>The transmission electron microscope (TEM) images of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0002">2</a>a,b) verify that the wealth of macropores and micropores generated during calcination are interconnected in the interior of the PCNFs and that the thickness of the carbon shells of the PCNFs is around 20 nm. It can be clearly observed that the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs with particle sizes ranging from 12 to 20 nm are uniformly distributed across the hollow interior of these PCNFs with a peapod-like structure (Figure S1 in the Supporting Information). By comparison, electrospinning under the same conditions was applied to a solution of PVA-FeCl<sub>3</sub> without the presence of metal azide in the precursor, and no porous structure was observed in the as-synthesized CNFs after the identical calcination process (Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0002">2</a>d; Figure S2, Supporting Information), with the product denoted as Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs. Additionally, most Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs were grown on the surfaces of the CNFs with an average particle size approaching 30 nm in Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs, which highlights the important role of lithium azide in generating hierarchical mesopores and macropores, and preventing the in situ growth of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs during the calcination process. Furthermore, it should be noted that, during the synthesis of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs, the optimization of the time and the temperature for carbonization is also of great importance in inhibiting the aggregation of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs and their growth on the surfaces of CNFs (Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0002">2</a>e,f; Figure S3, Supporting Information). The uniform distribution of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs inside the PCNFs could not only accommodate their volume expansion during repeated discharge/charge processes but also prevent their aggregation along the porous carbon shell. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs displays clear lattice fringes with interplanar spacing of 0.25 nm, corresponding to the (110) and <img alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201701561:smll201701561-math-0002" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/363dc307-22be-429a-b6d3-765f3796b099/smll201701561-math-0002.png"> planes of α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> with an angle of 60° between these two types of planes (Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0002">2</a>b), while no obvious lattice fringes of carbon are observed, indicating the amorphous nature of the carbon. The porous structure of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs was further investigated by nitrogen physisorption measurements. As shown in Figure S4 in the Supporting Information, the adsorption/desorption isotherms at 77 K represent typical type IV hysteresis loops at relative pressure between 0.5 and 1.0, indicating the existence of a large number of mesopores. The pore sizes of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs (the inset of Figure S4 in the Supporting Information) are mainly distributed between 2 and 50 nm, which coincides well with the TEM results. A large surface area of 237.5 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> is revealed according to the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller method. The presence of abundant porous structure is beneficial for enhancing the electrode/electrolyte interfaces and allowing easy access of electrolyte.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201701561-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3de558c3-8c6b-4037-9c2c-f4117c0651c5/smll201701561-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3de558c3-8c6b-4037-9c2c-f4117c0651c5/smll201701561-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/07a2ec5b-ba73-4652-a20e-d8fb0158abb5/smll201701561-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3de558c3-8c6b-4037-9c2c-f4117c0651c5/smll201701561-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201701561-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201701561" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) TEM images of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs. The inset of panel (b) shows an HRTEM image of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs. c) Elemental mapping of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs. TEM images of d) the as-synthesized Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs treated by carbonization at e) 700 °C and f) 750 °C for 1 h.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The purity and crystalline phase of the samples were further characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD). As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0003">3</a>a, all of the diffraction peaks of both Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs can be indexed to α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. The average crystallite size of the thus formed α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> was calculated to be ≈15 nm, derived from the Debye–Scherrer equation on the (200) peak, which is in good agreement with the TEM observations. By comparison, the diffraction peaks of α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> are stronger and sharper in Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs, indicating the increase in the crystallite size without the addition of the porogen agent. The chemical composition and valences of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs were subsequently investigated by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and Raman spectroscopy (Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0003">3</a>b). In the XPS survey spectra (Figure S5a, Supporting Information), the characteristic peaks of Fe, C, and O can be clearly observed to be coupled with the presence of N with a content of 3.6 wt%, which is attributed to the reaction between CNFs and Li<sub>3</sub>N resulting from the decomposition of LiN<sub>3</sub> during the carbonization process.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0011_R_d50504752e761" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></span> Three fitted peaks in the XPS N 1s spectrum are ascribed to pyridinic-N (398.3 eV), pyrrolic-N (399.7 eV), and graphitic-N (401.1 eV),<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0012_R_d50504752e763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Mater.">12</a></span> respectively, and the high-resolution C 1s spectrum (Figure S5, Supporting Information) further verifies the formation of C<span class="icomoon"></span>N bonds (285.5 eV). In contrast, no peaks were detected in the N 1s spectra for Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs owing to the absence of lithium azide (Figure S6, Supporting Information). Furthermore, energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy elemental mapping (Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0002">2</a>c) validates the uniform distributions of Fe, O, C, and N in the as-prepared Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs. These results demonstrate that lithium azide could serve as not only a porogen agent but also as an N source during the thermal treatment of PVA nanofibers, leading to the formation of N-doped PCNFs. It has been well established that nitrogen doping is an effective route to improving the Li<sup>+</sup>(Na<sup>+</sup>) ion storage capacities of carbons and enhancing the electronic conductivity.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0013_R_d50504752e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></span> Moreover, two major peaks in the Fe 2p core–shell XPS spectrum at around 711 and 725 eV are attributed to Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of Fe<sup>3+</sup>, respectively, with two satellite peaks appearing on their high binding-energy side (≈8 eV), which is the fingerprint of the electronic structure of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0014_R_d50504752e793" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nano Res.">14</a></span> This finding justifies the formation of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and the absence of Fe<sup>2+</sup> in the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs. Raman spectra of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs also exhibit the characteristic peaks of α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (<i>A</i><sub>1g</sub>: 214 cm<sup>−1</sup>; <i>E</i><sub>g</sub>: 273 and 384 cm<sup>−1</sup>). The other two broad peaks at about 1348 and 1587 cm<sup>−1</sup> are indexed to the defect-induced mode and the <i>E</i><sub>2g2</sub> graphitic mode of amorphous carbon, respectively, with an intensity ratio (<i>R</i> = <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) of 0.97, which is consistent with the TEM results. This result indicates the presence of abundant defects and vacancies in the as-formed N-PCNFs, which could not only facilitate the diffusion of Li<sup>+</sup> (Na<sup>+</sup>) ions but also offer more intercalation sites for Li (Na)-storage.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0015_R_d50504752e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano Lett.">15</a></span> The content of carbon in Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs was determined to be 39.5% by weight loss according to the thermal analysis results (Figure S7, Supporting Information). It should be noted that the negligible weight decrease of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs below 200 °C could be attributed to the evaporation of adsorbed water.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0016_R_d50504752e855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a></span></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201701561-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/80a26d93-f1b5-4d31-adbe-409a5aac2d9b/smll201701561-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/80a26d93-f1b5-4d31-adbe-409a5aac2d9b/smll201701561-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e4dc6039-2485-4a02-8c07-d1a362d3480b/smll201701561-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/80a26d93-f1b5-4d31-adbe-409a5aac2d9b/smll201701561-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201701561-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201701561" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns and b) Raman spectra of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs. High-resolution c) Fe 2p and d) N 1s spectra of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll201701561-sec-0040">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll201701561-sec-0040-title">2.2 Lithium Ion Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs as anode material for LIBs was first investigated by cyclic voltammograms (CV). The CV curves of the initial four cycles for Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0004">4</a>a) were collected at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>−1</sup> within the potential range of 0.01–3 V. In the initial cathodic scan, two reduction peaks at around 0.89 and 0.55 V, corresponding to the Li<sup>+</sup> insertion into Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> to form Fe and Li<sub>2</sub>O and the formation of SEI films, respectively, are observed.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0017_R_d50504752e922" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#smll201701561-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0018_R_d50504752e922" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Phys. Chem. C">18</a></span> On the other hand, the oxidation peak at around 1.62 V in the anodic sweep is attributed to the reversible oxidation of Fe to Fe<sup>3+</sup>.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0018_R_d50504752e926" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Phys. Chem. C">18</a></span> It is worth noting that the CV curves of the subsequent cycles are almost overlapping, suggesting excellent reversibility of the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs membrane during the repeated lithium insertion/extraction processes. Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0004">4</a>b presents galvanostatic discharge–charge profiles (1st, 2nd, 50th, and 100th cycles) of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs membrane at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. A distinct voltage plateau at ≈0.88 V is clearly observed, in good agreement with the above CV results. It should be noted that the electrode is able to deliver a high discharge and charge capacity of 1953 and 1332 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to an initial Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 68.2%. The large irreversible capacity is attributed to the consumption of lithium ions during the formation of SEI film. In the second and third cycles, the CE significantly increases to 94.7% and 97.6%, suggesting the rapid stabilization of the SEI layer, and it remains at nearly 100% thereafter. Interestingly, the reversible capacity of the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode slightly increases to 1524 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the first 21 cycles, which has also been found in various nanostructured metal oxide electrodes. It could be derived from the possible activation of the electrode materials,<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0019_R_d50504752e952" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></span> which could lead to the gradual wetting of the electrolyte into the pores of N-PCNFs and gradually enhance the utilization rate of active materials, and the progressive generation of electrochemical active polymeric gel-like films,[<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0009_R_d50504752e954" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Interfaces ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Sci. Rep. J. Power Sources">9</a></span>] which is caused by the decomposition of electrolytes on the surfaces of the continuously pulverized particles due to the electrochemical grinding effect. Remarkably, after 100 cycles at the rate of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, a reversible capacity as high as 1434 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was sustained (Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0004">4</a>c), which represents a minor increase in the capacity compared with the second cycle (1411 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). The reversible capacity of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs is obviously higher than the theoretical value of iron oxide, which could be commonly observed for nanostructured iron oxide electrodes. It is mainly attributed to the following reasons. First, the significant reduction of particle size of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> leads to the increase of the interfacial area at the Li<sub>2</sub>O/Fe interface. It could significantly enhance the charge interfacial storage and hence the electrochemical capacity based on the two-phase capacitive behavior of the Li<sub>2</sub>O/Fe interface, which could enable the storage of Li ions on the Li<sub>2</sub>O compound while electrons are localized on the Fe side.[<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0009_R_d50504752e981" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Interfaces ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Sci. Rep. J. Power Sources">9</a></span>] Furthermore, the excessive lithium storage in metal oxide could result from the generation of LiOH and its subsequent reversible reaction with Li to generate Li<sub>2</sub>O and LiH owing to the possible interaction between them with N-PCNFs.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0020_R_d50504752e986" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Mater.">20</a></span> In addition, the activation of nanostructured Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs during the cycling charge–discharge process may result in the loss of crystallinity of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> to form an amorphous-like structure, which could further improve the Li diffusion kinetics and the reversible capacity.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0021_R_d50504752e997" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a></span> By comparison, the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs electrodes can only deliver initial capacities of 1235 and 1090 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, with a corresponding CE of 56.5% and 55.1% for the initial discharging–charging process (Figure S8, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201701561-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a0e4c061-d17c-4cdf-84e3-ed930b3f2d46/smll201701561-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a0e4c061-d17c-4cdf-84e3-ed930b3f2d46/smll201701561-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dee1daec-b4ff-4c45-b449-5600e15684e7/smll201701561-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a0e4c061-d17c-4cdf-84e3-ed930b3f2d46/smll201701561-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201701561-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201701561" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode in LIBs. a) Cyclic voltammograms for the initial four cycles of the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode at a scanning rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Galvanostatic discharge–charge profiles for selected cycles at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Cycling performance at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and Coulombic efficiency of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode, with Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs included for comparison. d) Rate performance at different current densities from 100 to 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, with Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs included for comparison. e) Cycling performance at a current density of 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The rate capability of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode was subsequently investigated by charging and discharging at various current densities from 100 to 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0004">4</a>d). After increasing the current density to 1000 and 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacity at these current densities could still reach 1120 and 1002 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. In strong contrast, the capacities of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs and pure Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs rapidly decreased to only 205 and 24 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. More importantly, a stable capacity of 1521 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> could still be delivered when the current density was reduced back to 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, so that there was almost no capacity loss, which demonstrates the strong tolerance of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs toward rapid Li ion insertion and extraction. Furthermore, the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode exhibited an impressive cycle life with a high reversible capacity of 958 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, a high CE of around 100%, and 0.015% decay per cycle from the second cycle at a high current density of 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0004">4</a>e). The discharge capacity of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode after 1000 cycles at 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> is still much higher than that of commercial graphite anodes with superior retention of 93% of the 10th cycle discharge capacity, which provides further evidence for its superior reversibility.</p>
            
            <p>In order to explore the intrinsic electrochemical and kinetic mechanisms of the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy was conducted after the first cycle (Figure S9, Supporting Information). All the Nyquist plots of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs, Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs, and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs consist of a semicircle in the medium frequency region, corresponding to the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), followed by a straight line in the low frequency region. It can be clearly observed that Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs exhibit semicircles with a much smaller diameter than Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs, suggesting much higher electronic conductivity. By fitting the Nyquist plots with an equivalent circuit, the value of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> for Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs was calculated to be 58.5 Ω, which is much lower than that of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs (65.1 Ω). This confirms that the doping with N could further increase the electronic conductivity of CNFs. More importantly, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> for Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs is reduced to only 32 Ω after 200 cycles, which is attributed to the electrode–electrolyte activation in the initial cycles, while this value is increased to 136.3 Ω for Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs. This result demonstrates the stable interface between the electrolyte and the active materials during repeated lithiation and delithiation processes, leading to the stable cycling performance of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs (Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0004">4</a>e). As revealed by the SEM and TEM results (Figure S10, Supporting Information), Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs still preserves its original morphology, and all the hollow interiors of N-PCNFs were occupied by the swollen and pulverized Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs, which demonstrates that the volume and the mechanical properties of the hollow N-PCNFs are large enough to accommodate the volume expansion of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs during long-term lithiation processes, thus ensuring high cycling stability. By comparison, the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs exposed on the surfaces of CNFs in Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs have collapsed and aggregated to some extent after cycling, which is attributed to the stress and strain induced by discharge and charge processes during cycling, which could be the reason for the rapid capacity decay of this electrode.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll201701561-sec-0050">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll201701561-sec-0050-title">2.3 Sodium Ion Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The novel structural features that are improving the electrochemical properties of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> encouraged us to further examine the performance of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs as anode material for SIBs. The sodium storage behavior of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode was first investigated by CV in the voltage range of 0.01–3 V at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> to clarify the electrochemical reactions involved. During the first cathodic scan process, the broad peak at ≈0.89 V could be attributed to the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> to form α-Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, while the peak at ≈0.52 V is ascribed to the conversion of the α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> accompanied by the formation of the SEI layer.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0022_R_d50504752e1258" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Power Sources">22</a></span> In the following two cycles, only a broad cathodic peak at ≈0.66 V was observed, which is assigned to the conversion process of α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>.<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0022_R_d50504752e1265" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Power Sources">22</a></span> The weak oxidation peaks at ≈0.88 and ≈1.40 V could be attributed to a two-step oxidation of Fe<sup>0</sup> (i.e., Fe<sup>0</sup> → Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> → Fe<sup>3+</sup>, respectively).<span><a href="#smll201701561-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201701561-bib-0023_R_d50504752e1278" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a></span> <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0005">5</a>b presents the charge/discharge curves of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs at the current density of 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. A first discharge capacity of 1542 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a charge capacity of 1148 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> were observed, corresponding to an initial CE of 74.5%. In the second cycle, the discharge and charge capacities drop to 1035 and 927 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to a CE of 89.6%. After that, the CE increases quickly to ≈100%, and remarkably, the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode still exhibits a discharge capacity of 806 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles, which is around 78% retention of the second cycle capacity (Figure <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0005">5</a>c). By comparison, the first cycle discharge capacity of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs electrode was only 186 and 663 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, and the discharge capacity of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs decreased to only 319 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 5 cycles (Figure S11, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201701561-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4a0b080f-f88e-431c-addb-95b122698c8e/smll201701561-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4a0b080f-f88e-431c-addb-95b122698c8e/smll201701561-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ca8c92b3-650d-439a-8e61-9928cdf03e89/smll201701561-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4a0b080f-f88e-431c-addb-95b122698c8e/smll201701561-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201701561-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201701561" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode in SIBs. a) Cyclic voltammograms for the initial three cycles of the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles for selected cycles at a current density of 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Cycling performance at a current density of 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and Coulombic efficiency of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode, with Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs included for comparison. d) Rate performance at different current densities from 200 to 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, with Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs included for comparison. e) Cycling performance at a current density of 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Furthermore, the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode exhibited excellent high-rate performance as anode material in SIBs. The average specific capacities of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode are 904, 716, 619, 498, and 456 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 200, 500, 800, 1000, and 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which is much higher than for the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs and the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@CNFs electrode. Moreover, the discharge capacity of the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode can recover to 828 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> when the current density returns to 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, approaching the value in the early cycles. More importantly, the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode can still deliver a high capacity of 396 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at the high current density of 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> after 1500 cycles, with a fading rate of only 0.007% per cycle after the first cycle and a CE approaching 100%, indicating the superior high-rate cycling stability.</p>
            
            <p>To the best of our knowledge, this is among the best cycling stability performances reported to date in Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-based materials for both lithium (Table S1, Supporting Information) and sodium (Table S2, Supporting Information) storage. The exceptional electrochemical performance of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs is mainly attributed to its unique structural features (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201701561-fig-0006">6</a>). First, the homogeneous distribution can effectively reduce the dissolution and aggregation of the active Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs during electrochemical processes, and the large void space between the N-PCNFs and the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs coupled with their ultrasmall nature could efficiently accommodate the large volumetric expansion of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> during lithiation and, especially, sodiation. Second, the uniform Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs with an average size of 16 nm could significantly improve the utilization rate of active materials and reduce the absolute strain during the electrochemical reactions. Third, the continuous and porous 1D N-doped carbon nanofibers have exhibited remarkable properties toward improving the electrochemical performance of LIBs and SIBs, which is attributed to their short diffusion distance, excellent stress tolerance, and good conductivity. Moreover, the 3D hierarchical porous architecture provides a large electrode–electrolyte contact area and short Li-ion transport pathways through facilitating electrolyte contact with the pores, which could further improve the electrochemical activity of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs uniformly encapsulated in N-PCNFs. Finally, the self-supported electronic active materials can serve as an efficient electronic current collector, thereby eliminating the requirement for conductive additives, polymetric binders, and metallic current collectors that generally amounts to a total of ≈10% of the cell weight in the conventional negative electrode structure. Therefore, this structure could further enhance the power density of batteries and simplify the cell packing process.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201701561-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5cc42644-eb9d-4342-8be9-ec6ab0f45c50/smll201701561-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5cc42644-eb9d-4342-8be9-ec6ab0f45c50/smll201701561-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1629e17b-d6d3-498f-b2ca-23f2a80db0d7/smll201701561-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5cc42644-eb9d-4342-8be9-ec6ab0f45c50/smll201701561-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201701561-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201701561" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the charge/discharge processes of the Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs electrode.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201701561-sec-0060">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201701561-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, an efficient approach to realizing the homogeneous distribution of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> NPs in the hollow interior of N-doped porous carbon nanofibers is demonstrated via a facile and scalable electrospinning technique to produce binder- and current collector-free anode materials for LIBs and SIBs. The distinctive structural features of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@N-PCNFs could not only effectively accommodate the volume changes of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and prevent its pulverization/aggregation during long-term cycling processes but also facilitate the transportation of lithium (sodium) ions and electrons through the electrode, leading to significantly improved electrochemical activity toward lithium and sodium storage. This study offers a new method for designing electrode materials for LIBs and SIBs with high and stable performance. In addition, this novel strategy could be extended to the fabrication of various types of functional nanomaterials for wide applications in energy storage and catalysis.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
